10:T744fa,<h3>Further Extracted Evidence</h3>
<ul>
<li><h2>User  So let&#39;s imagine we were going to do a study of a few terms like nous, logos, and anthropos. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: 2026-03-05_corpus_hermeticum_data_project:page_1">[eref]</span></h2>
</li>
<li><h2>Assistant  For a focused study of terms like &quot;nous,&quot; &quot;logos,&quot; and &quot;anthropos,&quot; you&#39;d start by building a term-specific index. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: 2026-03-05_corpus_hermeticum_data_project:page_1">[eref]</span></h2>
</li>
<li>Once indexed, apply co-occurrence and semantic network analysis—this will show how &quot;nous&quot; might cluster around particular metaphysical treatises, how &quot;logos&quot; interrelates with cosmological contexts, and how &quot;anthropos&quot; is situated in discussions of human nature. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: 2026-03-05_corpus_hermeticum_data_project:page_1">[eref]</span></li>
<li>(49) Nous nous permettons de renvoyer ici à A. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_11">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Voir aussi dans l’alchimie arabo-latine, par exemple dans le Lumen luminum ou De perfecto magisterio attribué à Râzî ou à Aristote où nous lisons que « le magistère accompli consiste à r <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_12">[eref]</span></li>
<li>126 ANTOINE CALVET comme nous le verrons plus loin. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_13">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous avons vu plus haut que le maître dominicain portait un regard critique sur l’or alchimique tel que le fabriquaient les alchimistes de son temps. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_14">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Cette thèse, nous le verrons plus bas, sera souvent citée et commentée par les alchimistes se réclamant de son enseignement. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_14">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous renvoyons sur ce sujet au travail de JECK, « Materia, forma substantialis, transmutatio », p. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_14">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Leur examen nous instruit sur la manière dont les alchimistes percevaient leur art et sur l’écho qu’ils en espéraient dans la société de leur temps. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_16">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Les textes que nous étudions dans les chapitres suivants en portent témoignage. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_16">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous l’avons lue dans la version de Borgnet (Libellus de Alchimia) 79 et dans celle de sa traduction anglaise, annotée et commentée par Virginia Heines 80. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_16">[eref]</span></li>
<li>« Nous sommes, [dit-elle dans le cas d’édition comme celle de Borgnet], en présence d’une Semita recta moins affirmée, moins colorée, un peu aseptisée en quelque sorte ». <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_18">[eref]</span></li>
<li>De telles variations ne doivent pas nous étonner : le statut de ces manuscrits reste marqué par une évolution quasi naturelle, les premiers témoins livrant un état du texte influencé par l’ambiance d’école, voire de secte philosophique, dans laquelle a été conçue la Semita ; plus tard, l’image d’un Albert, sommité scolastique, inclina les copistes à effacer la part d’ombre et de mystère des premières versions. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_18">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Si nous la rejetons aujourd’hui, il y a tout lieu de chercher dans l’environnement immédiat du maître dominicain l’origine de ce texte, écrit à une époque où l’alchimie n’était pas encore cette science attaquée par l’Inquisition – car liée aux démons 97 –, mais un art efficace pour pénétrer les arcanes de la nature, selon l’idée même d’Albert. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_18">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Dans un premier temps, nous tentons d’établir les idées et l’apport d’Albert à la question de l’alchimie. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_2">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Puis, nous étudions les principaux traités alchimiques qui lui ont été attribués. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_2">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous constatons dès lors la grande influence du De mineralibus sur la plupart des textes du corpus, à l’exception des recettes étrangères aux idées albertiniennes. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_2">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Voici ce que le pseudo-Albert écrit : À partir de tous les livres de notre science et des choses cachées dans les secrets, obtenus par une diligente recherche (en laissant de coté les aspects divins des causes et les causes des choses divines), qui affirment ou nient la science que nous appelons alchimique, à ta requête et pour que tu n’ignores pas les choses de ce genre expérimentées par nous, nous les avons condensés pour toi en trois écrits  132. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_22">[eref]</span></li>
<li>66 : « Chaque fois que nous rencontrons cette théorie corpusculaire dans un context <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_22">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous remercions Jean-Marc Mandosio qui nous a aidé à traduire cette phrase. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_23">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Il nous a paru utile d’en relever les plus significatives, qui, par ailleurs, ont fécondé d’autres textes alchimiques du XIV e siècle, comme ceux du pseudo-Arnaud de Villeneuve, du pseudo-Lulle ou encore de John Dastin. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_25">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous n’avons rencontré cette phrase ni dans l’édition imprimée des Allegoriae (ed. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_26">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous ignorons tout de ce traité (De Thelesimo), mais la beauté de cet extrait brille de l’éclat du mystère : celui de la Création par le Dieu de la Genèse également commentée dans d’autres traités alchimiques 174. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_27">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Cependant, des travaux récents nous ont incités à reprendre le sujet, proposant une étude de ces textes alchimiques qui ont circulé sous le nom d’Albert au Moyen Âge, notre but étant de voir en quoi ces traités relèvent de sa pensée sur l’alchimie. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_3">[eref]</span></li>
<li>C’est ce point de vue que nous rappellerons dans les lignes suivantes. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_3">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Dans la seconde version, Bonaventure nous infor- mait qu’Albert « aurait reçu, lorsqu’il était encore vivant, de la part du Pape, le privilège “d’apprendre, connaître, examiner, et essayer toutes les sciences du bien et du mal, en louant les livres de vérité et en condamnant les livres de la fausseté et de l’erreur” ». <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_3">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous avons relevé parmi les plus significatifs : humiditas naturalis et radicalis 201, l’idée que l’élixir pénètre les métaux une fois purifiés sicut medicina venas 202, plus loin, que l’élixir ne « rend la santé » qu’aux métaux préparés de même qu’une médecine ne peut soigner un corps qu’adaptée à ce dernier en s’y mêlant 203. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_30">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Par conséquent, il en exclut toute caractérisation par la thèse gébérienne de la double nature, l’une manifeste, l’autre occulte 218, thèse que nous dépistons chez un Sedacer par exem- ple 219. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_32">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Désormais nous devons dire un mot de quelques-uns des textes ma- nuscrits, inédits à ce jour, ceux du moins qui restent à notre portée. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_33">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous avons sélectionné le Compositus de compositis dans sa version parisienne du XVI e siècle copiée par Oronce Finé, les Propositiones maxime in arte alchimiae et le Secre- torum tractatus. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_33">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Les autres écrits consultés sont des réceptaires auxquels nous réservons quelques commentaires rapides 224. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_33">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Puis, nous consacrons notre dernière partie à une analyse plus générale du corpus identifiant quelques grandes idées communes à tous ces textes qui lui assuraient une certaine homogéneité. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_33">[eref]</span></li>
<li>(224) Nous avons limité notre enquête à ces ouvrages, mais le corpus comprend d’autres textes : les Compendium de ortu et metallorum materia (ed. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_33">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous avons lu les Propositiones dans un manuscrit de la Bibliothèque nationale 254. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_36">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous avons là un exemple (255) TK, 1001. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_37">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous l’avons découvert dans l’édition imprimée de l’Artis auriferae 262. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_38">[eref]</span></li>
<li>C’est ainsi que nous comptons dans la liste de Pearl Kibre onze traités qui peuvent être considérés comme tels, dont un traité sur les Eaux (Duodecim aquae), un réceptaire alchimique qui connut un réel succès. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_40">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Cependant, si nous comparons deux recettes du pseudo-Albert, celle de l’Alkimia minor 280 et celle consignée par Léonard de Mauperg, nous observons des concordances qui en éclairent le processus. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_41">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous n’avons pas vu tous ces textes, loin de là, mais seulement quelques-uns parmi les plus marquants. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_42">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous avons lu le Breve Breviarum (TK, 180), in Sanioris Medicinae Magistri D. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_42">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Ni dans l’édition de 1541 291, ni dans celle de Borgnet, ni dans celle de Wyckoff, nous n’avons rencontré ce petit poème. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_44">[eref]</span></li>
<li>De plus, cette clausule ne nous renseigne pas sur une influence directe du De minera- libus sur le Testamentum. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_44">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous résumons ici quelques aspects de ce traité important 299. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_45">[eref]</span></li>
<li>En revanche, l’héritage d’Albert nous semble pleinement assumé, quand il recueillit dans la mythologie et chez Ovide des exemples pour illustrer et donner plus d’éclat à son propos. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_46">[eref]</span></li>
<li>CONCLUSION Nous avons vu que l’alchimie fut imputée à l’œuvre d’Albert de son vivant. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_46">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Ces traits distinctifs articulent la plupart des textes que nous avons étudiés. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_47">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous sommes là au cœur d’une alchimie scolastique qui s’éver- tuait à transmettre un enseignement de haute volée, instruit, fondée en théorie et en pratique. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_47">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous étudions plus loin cette légende que recoupe celle d’Albert professeur alchimiste de Roger Bacon. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: archives_dhistoire_doctrinale_et_littéraire_du_moyen_âge_vol_79_iss_1_calvet_antoine_author_-_the_alchemy_of_pse:page_6">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Reasoned dis- course [logos] does <not> get to the truth, but mind [nous] is powerful, and, when it has been guided by reason [logos] up to a point, it has the means to get <as far as> the truth. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: aries_2015-jan_01_vol_15_iss_2_how_hermetic_was_renaissance_hermetism_hanegraaff_wouter_j2015_january_011011:page_10">[eref]</span></li>
<li>He continues by stating that ‘especially problematic is the large family of words cognate with the noun nous or “mind”; e.g., noeō, noēma, noēsis, noētos, ennoia, dianoia, pronoia, etc.; and with the noun gnōsis or “knowledge”: e.g. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: aries_2015-jan_01_vol_15_iss_2_how_hermetic_was_renaissance_hermetism_hanegraaff_wouter_j2015_january_011011:page_11">[eref]</span></li>
<li>As a further result, there is no longer an opposition between “rea- soned discourse” (‘logos’) and the superior power of “mind” (‘nous’) either:32 Ficino simply starts a new sentence which states that the human mind is pow- erful, and repeats once again that Hermes’s discourse will lead to the truth: ‘Mens quoque ampla et a sermone ad certum quiddam deducta veritatem attigit’. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: aries_2015-jan_01_vol_15_iss_2_how_hermetic_was_renaissance_hermetism_hanegraaff_wouter_j2015_january_011011:page_12">[eref]</span></li>
<li>One who has understood it can understand nothing else, norcanonewhohaslookedonitlookonanythingelseorhearofanything 32 On the relation between logos and nous, cf. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: aries_2015-jan_01_vol_15_iss_2_how_hermetic_was_renaissance_hermetism_hanegraaff_wouter_j2015_january_011011:page_12">[eref]</span></li>
<li>A ceux donc, qui entendent les choses, qui nous ont esté dictes de Dieu, elles seront <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: aries_2015-jan_01_vol_15_iss_2_how_hermetic_was_renaissance_hermetism_hanegraaff_wouter_j2015_january_011011:page_24">[eref]</span></li>
<li>76 ‘Le tiers degré est cognoistre ou entendre, lequel est donné de Dieu a l’homme qui con- tinue liberalement en soy l’exercise de la foy, par lequel il entre si avant en cognoissance des choses divines, par l’ouverture de ce grand exemplaire, duquel nous avons souvent parlé, &amp; dans lequel l’homme voit ce, que bouche ne peut dire, œil n’a veu, &amp; cœur n’a pensé, comme sainct Pol la veu en son ravissement, Moïse en la montaigne, &amp; Mercure dans sa vision’ (ibid., 323). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: aries_2015-jan_01_vol_15_iss_2_how_hermetic_was_renaissance_hermetism_hanegraaff_wouter_j2015_january_011011:page_25">[eref]</span></li>
<li>peuvent facilement estre vaincus &amp; subiuguez, voire sans aucun sommeil corporel, par les vertus intelligibles, conduites d’une bonne volonté &amp; affection ardante, comme en c’est endroit nous voyons estre advenu à Mercure, sans aucun sommeil corporel un amortissement &amp; suppression de sens’ (ibid., 2). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: aries_2015-jan_01_vol_15_iss_2_how_hermetic_was_renaissance_hermetism_hanegraaff_wouter_j2015_january_011011:page_26">[eref]</span></li>
<li>1): Asclepius, Tat, Agathodaimon, Nous and Ammon are  prominent as interlocutors or authorities in the seventeen Greek  Hermetica and the Asclepius; Isis and others appear in the fragments  preserved by Stobaeus; FR II, 5-6. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_180">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Griffith, proposed p-eime-n-re, &quot;the knowledge of  Re,&quot; which accords with section 2, below, if nous (&quot;mind&quot;) is taken  as equivalent to eime (&quot;knowledge&quot;) and Re, the sun god, is authentia  (&quot;sovereignty&quot;). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_181">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Ennoias, the first word of  this phrase, points immediately to the problem of translating the  Hermetic vocabulary of perception, cognition and intuition; especially  problematic is the large family of words cognate with the noun nous  or &quot;mind&quot;: e.g., noeo, noema, noesis, noetos, ennoia, dianoia, pronoia,  etc.; and with the noun gnosis or &quot;knowledge&quot;: e.g., gignosko, gnorizb,  prognosis, diagnosis, etc. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_182">[eref]</span></li>
<li><ol start="957">
<li>says that &quot;the philosophical concept nous is  brought into close connection with mystical religious ideas, though  with an ambiguity of content that defies analysis.&quot; For related prob- lems, see below, notes on C.H. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_182">[eref]</span></li>
</ol>
</li>
<li>15, for the Iranian Ahura Mazda as nous; and Mahe, Hermes I,  13-14, for Authentikos Logos as the title of a treatise (NHC  VI.3)  preserved with Hermetic documents in the Nag Hammadi codices. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_183">[eref]</span></li>
<li>When he wanted to  laugh the third time, Nous or Phrenes [Mind or Wits] appeared holding  a heart, because of the sharpness of the god. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_183">[eref]</span></li>
<li>the father:  For ho de nous pater theos, NF I, 9, has &quot;ton  Nous est le Dieu Pere,&quot; but see also Dodd, Bible, pp. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_188">[eref]</span></li>
<li>1.8  In your mind: NF I, 9, has &quot;dans le Nous,&quot; but see p. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_188">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Like other entities {nous, anthropos, logos) in this discourse  and elsewhere in the Hermetica, God&#39;s Counsel (boule) may be under- stood as hypostasized; the ordinary psychological meaning of such  language permits us also to understand certain entities as existing both  in man as faculties of soul or mind and also on a higher plane as  autonomous and divine principles. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_189">[eref]</span></li>
<li>must bend to his will a material that is to some  extent recalcitrant.&quot; In Middle Platonism, &quot;the Demiourgos came to  be seen as a second god, Intellect (nous), the agent or logos of the  Supreme God,&quot; according to Dillon, Middle Platonists, pp. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_190">[eref]</span></li>
<li><ol start="29">
<li>on the Demiurge, Nous  and creation, and explains them as results of variations in Egyptian  cosmology. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_190">[eref]</span></li>
</ol>
</li>
<li>(who) is mindful:  For ho ennousy NF I, 13, has &quot;celui qui a l&#39;intellect,&quot;  but Nock (1947), p. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_198">[eref]</span></li>
<li>645, gives &quot;with Nous&quot;; NF inserts the definite  article, following Turnebus. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_198">[eref]</span></li>
<li>6-7) distinguishes between mind  (nous) and reason (logos) as between intuition and ratiocination; only  the former, a special gift from God, leads to saving knowledge (gnosis);  the latter, shared by all men, implies both discursive reasoning and  the expression of reasoning in speech. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_219">[eref]</span></li>
<li>1.22-3 and XII.3-4, where all people  have at least the capability of expressing mind; and Ferguson (Scott  IVF, 367), who makes the opposition between nous and logos parallel  to the difference between the pneumatikoi  and the psuchikoi, citing  Reitzenstein, HMR,  pp. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_219">[eref]</span></li>
<li>mind  who understands:  For nous men nooumenos NF I, 65, 69, n. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_228">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Ferguson interprets the passage in light of  Stoic doctrine on a &quot;common reason&quot; (koinos logos) joining nous and  aisthesis (&quot;sensation&quot;). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_237">[eref]</span></li>
<li>the instrument:  The supplicant in NHC VI.6.30- 1, calls himself &quot;the instrument&quot; of God&#39;s spirit and calls nous the  &quot;plectrum&quot; of the divinity; below, C.H. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_239">[eref]</span></li>
<li>39), who has &quot;la sensation  ne se produit qu&#39;en dependance de l&#39;objet qui fait impression sur  nous&quot; for aisthesis men gar ginetai tou epikratountos, explains the Stoic  notion that the soul in sensing material objects is altered by the effluvia  that it receives from them; i.e., the soul is somehow &quot;mastered&quot; by  material objects when it senses them, though it is free when it thinks  or reasons about them. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_245">[eref]</span></li>
<li>79, has &quot;intellect serviteur&quot; for nous  huperetikos-, on the latter term, see above, section 17. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_251">[eref]</span></li>
<li><ol start="33">
<li>believes that the Nous who addresses Hermes here is actually Poiman- dres, and Festugiere (NF I, 143, n. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_252">[eref]</span></li>
</ol>
</li>
<li>Scott II, 291-2, thinks that Nous  replaced Agathodaimon or Khnum. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_252">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nock (NF I, 178) suggests that nous is the implied subject  of the sentence &quot;If there . <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_263">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The animal identified  most closely with Amun was the ram; another was the serpent, a god  called Kneph and Agathodaimon in Greek and identified both as nous  and as demiourgos. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_287">[eref]</span></li>
<li><ol start="63">
<li>correctly associates  sensus with nous and usually translates it by &quot;intellect en donnant a ce <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_303">[eref]</span></li>
</ol>
</li>
<li>divinity:  On nous, gnosis and divinization, see above:  C.H. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_305">[eref]</span></li>
<li>aether:  On sensus as &quot;consciousness&quot; see above,  section 3, and on the Peripatetic sources of the doctrine that the  quintessence rather than the four elements is the material fundament  of psuche or nous, see Scott III, 38-42; NF II, 363, nn. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_307">[eref]</span></li>
<li>thought, consciousness, memory, foresight  (animus,  sensus,  memoria,  providentia);  Ferguson links sensus, in its first appearance here, with nous, but he  connects the second instance of sensus with aisthesis (&quot;sensation&quot;),  basing his analysis on Philo, Questions on Genesis 3.5, 4.3, and Who is  the Heir 125-32. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_310">[eref]</span></li>
<li>gifts: For sensu, disciplina, intellegentia (&quot;consciousness,  learning and understanding&quot;), Festugiere has &quot;d&#39;intellect, de science  et d&#39;entendement,&quot; explaining that they probably correspond to nous,  episteme and gnosis or logos; for mentes (&quot;minds&quot;) he has &quot;ames,&quot; in  agreement with Ferguson. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_315">[eref]</span></li>
<li>X.9-10; God&#39;s gift of nous raises humans above the  level of episteme and puts them on the path to gnosis. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_315">[eref]</span></li>
<li>IV.3-4, on which people have nous and logos and what  these terms imply. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_316">[eref]</span></li>
<li>152, explains the point  of the imagery: sensus (&quot;consciousness&quot; or nous) is a faculty of intuition, <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_316">[eref]</span></li>
<li>total consciousness:  As in general, &quot;consciousness&quot; in this paragraph  represents sensus, but it should be understood that the Greek term  behind the Latin may be nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_336">[eref]</span></li>
<li>In fact, Festugiere suggests ho pas  nous for omnis . <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_336">[eref]</span></li>
<li>He also  warns that the meanings of key terms here (sensus, intellegentia, intellec- ts) are equivocal, so that &quot;the translation remains quite uncertain&quot;  and &quot;it is impossible to discover the author&#39;s true intent.&quot; Scott also  concludes that &quot;this paragraph is meaningless in the Latin.&quot; Nonethe- less, he maintains that the passage as a whole, beginning with the  words omnis ergo (&quot;total&quot;), distinguishes three levels of nous: divine,  cosmic and human. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_336">[eref]</span></li>
<li>1.1) and that sensu is nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_337">[eref]</span></li>
<li>while alive:  For haec sancta animalia  nuncupari  Festugiere has &quot;reconnaissent officiellement ces animaux sacres que  nous voyons,&quot; explaining that these last three words represent haec  and proposing &quot;declarent saints, comme nous le voyons, les animaux&quot;  as an alternative. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: brian_p_copenhaver_hermetica_the_greek_corpus_hermeticum_and_the_latin_asclepius_in_a_new_english_translation_with_note:page_342">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Some commentators take ἐν ᾧ to be impersonal, referring  back to Heikton and Kmeph as respectively the first noetic being and the first nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_148">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Mahé (HHE 1:50) reads ἐν so that Eikton is composed of the first noetic being and the  first nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_148">[eref]</span></li>
<li>When the demiurge creates the seven governors of  the heavenly bodies, “the logos of God immediately leapt up from the elements  that weigh downwards, up to the pure craftwork of Nature, and it united with  the demiurgical nous, for they were of the same essence.”194 There is actually  a near-exact parallel to this in the Memphite theology of Ptah (715–701 BCE):  “And great and important is Ptah, who gave life to all the gods and their kas as  well, through this heart and this tongue through which Horus and Thoth both  became Ptah.”195 Heart corresponds to mind, and tongue to logos. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_156">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Similarly, we  recall from Iamblichus that the demiurgic nous as Amun “brings into the light  the invisible power of the hidden logoi,” and as Ptah “infallibly and expertly  brings to perfection each thing in accordance with truth.” This means that the  logos (or logoi) hidden within the first nous is brought to light by the demiurgic  nous, who uses it to exert creative force on matter. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_156">[eref]</span></li>
<li>To repeat: The Hermetic source of Iamblichus: 0: Heikton (Heka), the preessential One 1: Kmeph, the Monad, self-begotten nous 2: Materiality 3: Amun-Ptah-Osiris, the demiurgic nous 4: Matter derived from Materiality made into spherical cosmos corresponds to the process of creation in the Poimandres: 0: Authentia, the preessential One 1: Poimandres, the first nous 2: Nature 3: The demiurge, second nous &amp; logos 4: Four elements derived from Nature made into spherical cosmos206 204    Jørgen Podemann Sørensen, “The Egyptian Background of the ἱερὸς λόγος (CH III),” in  Apocryphon Severini (ed. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_158">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Yet another formulation of the same structure, though  without any philosophical framework, can be found in the so-called Leiden  Kosmopoiia (PGM XIII.160ff.), where God laughs seven times, and begets 1:  Light-Radiance; 2: Primal Water; 3: Nous-Phrenes (= Hermes); 4: Generative  power and Procreation (Genna and Spora); 5: Fate (Moira); 6: Time (Kairos);  7: Soul.207 The first four of these are clearly reminiscent of the Hermetic nu- merological system. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_159">[eref]</span></li>
<li>I have  for the most part opted for “mind”, though sometimes I merely transliterate nous, espe- cially in cases where the term seems more technical. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_16">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Nous is only mentioned twice in the  text: the monarchical god is said to be an “imperishable nous,” and he calls  Hermes “the soul of my soul, and holy nous of my nous.”227 The primal human  in the Poimandres also became soul and nous in the body: “Nature received the  spirit from ether and brought forth bodies in the form of the human. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_164">[eref]</span></li>
<li>From life  and light the human became soul and nous.”228 There is thus some connection  between soul and nous in both treatises, and the latter term is treated as the  more divine. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_164">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Once more, the Hermetic system of Iamblichus can elucidate the relation- ship between soul and nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_164">[eref]</span></li>
<li>There is a possible reference to Poimandres in a saying by “our nous” (ibid. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_164">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The body increases and reaches perfection  due to nature, while soul fills up with nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_165">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Every man has a body and a soul, but not  every soul has nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_165">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Consequently there are two (types of) nous:  the one is divine and the other belongs to  soul. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_165">[eref]</span></li>
<li>DH VIII, 4231 The descent of the two types of souls reported by Iamblichus presents a strik- ing parallel to the descent of the primal human in the Poimandres, only there  the primal human derives from nous and during its descent assumes the au- thority (ἐξουσία) of the demiurge, receiving the rank (τάξις), nature (φύσις) or  energy (ἐνεργεία) of the heavenly spheres (CH I, 13–14). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_165">[eref]</span></li>
<li>All the same, the result is a hermaphroditic  embodied human with nous and soul (CH I, 17), possessing both the authority  of the demiurge and the rank, nature, or energy of the planets. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_165">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The  human soul is the median kind between gods and animals, and its mind, al- though originally divine, became human when it entered the body (§ 18: νοῦς …  ἀνθρώπινος ὢν τῇ οἰκήσει).264 There are two choices available to this soul: being  reverent turns it into something demonic and divine, and eventually trans- forms the whole soul into nous (§ 19).265 This must be the human soul that is  extolled in the conclusion of the treatise as the authentic human, the mortal  261      CH X, 8–9. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_172">[eref]</span></li>
<li>264    This possibly alludes to how the heavenly human, consisting of life and light, becomes  soul and nous when it enters the human body (CH I, 17). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_172">[eref]</span></li>
<li>on nous and psyche  after death. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_173">[eref]</span></li>
<li>While the first group of people seems to be doomed to a downward spiral of  bestial reincarnations, the second group can after several reincarnations tran- scend their human nature and be filled by the divine nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_174">[eref]</span></li>
<li>also DH IX, 4:  “Everything is visible to one who has Nous; who(ever) thinks of himself in Nous knows  himself and who(ever) knows himself knows everything” (Ամենայն ինչ տեսանելի է,  որ միտս ունի. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_226">[eref]</span></li>
<li>When presented with the story of the mixing bowl full of nous, quoted  above, Tat exclaims that he too wants to be immersed. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_227">[eref]</span></li>
<li>All others are incapable  of containing the nature of the good  because they are body and soul and  have no place that can contain the  good.119 It would seem that, as Festugière stated, “nous nous trouvons en plein  dualisme.”120 Those who call anything else good do so out of ignorance of  the true state of things, and indeed to do so is considered impious (§§ 15–16). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_233">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Since the appellation father is said to derive from his creation of  everything (§ 17), it also makes good sense to identify the god called good and  father with the demiurgic nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_235">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The revenge (τιμωρία) might thus allude to the avenging de- mons (τιμωροί δαίμονες), who can mete out their vengeance either in the aerial  Hades or in the body of those who are deprived of nous.135 Festugière points  out that demons are arrayed under the sun in CH XVI, and that demons are  put in charge of humans at their birth, according to their horoscope. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_236">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Qu’avons-nous appris? <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_236">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Les papyrus magiques nous l’avaient appris  déjà.”35 Festugière probably here refers to Papyrus Mimaut, the Greek magical  papyrus that contains the Hermetic Prayer of Thanksgiving and also includes  some spells in Old Coptic. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_24">[eref]</span></li>
<li>233    Interestingly, learning is here said to use mind as an instrument; this must again be the  lower, attendant nous, since it itself is said to use the body as an instrument, which can- not be said of the divine nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_258">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Because of these difficulties, Scott (2:247) proposed to  make nous the instrument of gnôsis, not epistêmê (which he thought came from a mar- ginal note), and emended ⟨ἐναντίος⟩ σώματι: nous as the opposite of body instead of its  instrument. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_258">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Mahé instead speaks of a progression where different mental faculties are  gained, starting with gnosis, proceeding with logos and culminating with nous.236  However, these three terms are incommensurate. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_259">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Gnosis is a form of knowl- edge, logos is the rational and discursive capacity, while nous is an insight that  is co-essential with God. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_259">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Although I would agree with Mahé that nous is at the  summit of the way, when the disciple attains divine nous in a mystic union  with God, logos is in fact possessed by most people, and cannot be a separate  stage of the teaching. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_259">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The knowledge of  the world can also be referred to as ἐπιστήμη, while the knowledge of God re- quires the acquisition of divine nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_259">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Le seul point  commun entre les trois écrits hermétiques de cette secte et l’ensemble  des enseignement philosophiques d’Hermès que nous connaissons par  ailleurs, c’est le commentaire des sentences de Trismégiste, dont la tra- dition préexiste à la formation de la Poimandresgemeinde et perdure  vraisemblablement en dehors d’elle, suivant des traditions qui n’ont rien  de gnostique.48 At the time he wrote this, Mahé considered the Gnostic Poimandresgemeinde  to be behind the dualistic, pessimistic initiatory treatises, while the monistic,  optimistic treatises were scholastic and probably not related to this group, as  Festugière had claimed earlier. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_26">[eref]</span></li>
<li>3.7, for the teaching of two types of soul  or nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_268">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Grese claims that CH XIII denies the unregenerate both  nous and psychê. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_272">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The question is then what it is that is reborn in nous? <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_272">[eref]</span></li>
<li>If we presuppose the teaching of a double  nous, the sensible understanding must be the human noetic faculty which Tat  possesses even before the rebirth, while the divine nous is only gained in the  rite of rebirth.77 In order to understand the generation in God one must first  obtain divine powers, in keeping with the saying of CH XI, 20: “If you have not  made yourself like God, you are not able to understand God; for like is (only)  understandable to like.”78 The rebirth, then, will make Tat divine, and thus able  to understand God. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_276">[eref]</span></li>
<li>77     Grese does not comment upon the use of νοεῖν in connection with the senses here, as it  fits poorly into his theory that nous only enters the picture after the rebirth. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_276">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Here, the herald sent down with the mixing bowl proclaimed to the hearts of  men that, in order to become divine, the heart which is capable should im- merse itself into the mixing bowl filled with nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_277">[eref]</span></li>
<li>82     CH VII, 2; the heart is a receptacle of light-nous <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_277">[eref]</span></li>
<li>A further allegoresis is made of the myth  relating the birth of Eros from the union of Penia and Poros in the garden of Zeus: Penia is  the soul of Zeus, who represents nous, and becomes deficient when she breaks away from  him into his garden. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_280">[eref]</span></li>
<li>268 Chapter 5 body and shutting down the bodily senses in CH XIII, the prerequisites for re- ceiving a vision, as is clear from CH I, 1.111 Naturally, silence is, if not necessary,  then at least conducive to achieving such a state, as cross-cultural studies of  mysticism tend to confirm.112 There is a further close parallel to this passage in the Armenian Definitions  of Hermes Trismegistus to Asclepius, which has generally been overlooked:  “Since nous conceives logos in silence, only (that) logos (which comes) from  silence and nous (is) salvation. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_284">[eref]</span></li>
<li>(But that) logos (which comes) from logos (is)  only perdition; for by (his body) man is mortal, but by logos (he is) immortal.”113  This sentence is a close parallel to CH I, 30, and also recalls the statement of  CH I, 6, that the union of logos and nous is life.114 If nous and silence give birth  to logos, silence as a feminine noun must here be the female partner to the  masculine nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_284">[eref]</span></li>
<li>In CH IX it is said that the human nous conceives good things when it re- ceives seed from God, and bad things when it receives its seed from lower de- mons. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_284">[eref]</span></li>
<li>This offspring of nous is called a noema or noesis, a thought that can  only be externalized by means of its brother, logos.115 The common pattern is  that only the purified human is capable of receiving the seed of the good from  above, and the offspring is in some way connected to divine logos. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_284">[eref]</span></li>
<li>They purify Tat for the articulation  or joining together (συνάρθρωσις) of the logos,120 and the combination of the  ten powers is said to constitute the noeric birth.121 Tat thus becomes both a  logos and a god and son of God, which makes him equivalent to the demiurgic  nous of CH I.122 Anna van den Kerchove notes the presence of the antonym  of the unusual noun συνάρθρωσις in CH I, 4.123 Here, an inarticulate cry (βοή  ἀσυνάρθρως) was uttered from the dark nature, which was then answered by  the descent of the creative holy word (CH I, 5: λόγος ἅγιος), also called a son of  God, emanating from the light (CH I, 6). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_285">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The rebirth mirrors divine  birth: The hypercosmic light-nous of the Poimandres, which is presumably si- lent (CH I, 4: εὔδιόν), begets the logos in response to the descending darkness,  just as, in humans, the descent and joining together of the divine powers as  116       Franz Cumont, “Le culte égyptien et le mysticisme de Plotin,” Mon. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_285">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Immediately this Silence  conceives and becomes pregnant and gives birth, assuredly in silence, and it  is Nus that she gives birth to, who resembles the Father and is his equal in  every respect.”125 Divine nous is thus born from silence, and this also has a  ritual counterpart, in that silence according to the Tripartite Tractate is a name  for baptism (NHC I 128,31).126 In Valentinianism we thus have an important  parallel where ritual mirrors protology, using many of the same terms, at about  the same time and in a similar religious milieu. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_286">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Later he informs us: “I see the All, and myself in  nous.” The doctrinal explanation of what is going on is also supplied: the si- lence constitutes a divine womb, in which the powers of God can descend and  transform the candidate to a new human. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_287">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The Nous-Aion in human form praises the universal Nous-Aion.172  The self-predication of Tat, that he has become omnipresent and trans- temporal, is not really a boast, then, as Grese suggests, but far closer to an  aretalogy. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_299">[eref]</span></li>
<li>And to life, justice; and to death, injustice.”188 Life, light and justice are three  of the ten divine powers in CH XIII, 8, and injustice is one of the avengers.189  It therefore seems likely that the source of Refutation, speaking for “the  Egyptians,” is Hermes,190 especially since the cosmogony resulting from the nu- merology is structurally similar to that of the Poimandres: as we have seen, nous  there corresponds to the monad, and the dark descending nature to the dyad.191  185     Iamblichus defends divination against someone—perhaps Porphyry—who “is attribut- ing a certain kind of deceptive nature, both omniform and versatile, which takes on the  forms of gods, daemons, and ghosts of the dead.” (Myst. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_303">[eref]</span></li>
<li>This is obscure, but pos- sibly means that the year-god, Aion, who is completed by the planetary circuits  (CH XI, 7), is a second monad, like the demiurge is a second nous.192 Although neither the Refutation nor CH XIII mention Aion in connection  with the monad-decad numerology, J.-P. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_304">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Mahé has pointed out that Aion could  be interpreted numerically as alpha, iota, and ôn, that is, the one which is ten.193  Indeed, Mahé postulates a Hermetic decadic cosmology, drawing upon CH I  and Disc.8–9, as well as the Eugnostos (NHC III,3; V,1) and Iamblichus, to show  that the upper three levels of being, the decad, ennead and ogdoad, are to be  identified respectively with the unborn god, the self-born nous of light and life,  and the born logos who is also the demiurgic nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_304">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The good is the eighth power, and must therefore represent the ogdoad,  but life and light are in the Hermetica used to describe both the monadic nous  of the ennead and the demiurgic nous of the ogdoad, and never the unborn  pre-essential one in the decad. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_304">[eref]</span></li>
<li>289 The Ritual of Rebirth The first sentence pays homage to the first nous of the Poimandres, consist- ing of light and life. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_305">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The womb of the next two sentences must belong to this  same nous, which is thus the androgynous father who conceives from his own  nature.195 The eternal (αἰώνιος) permanence seems to be born from the father  who conceives (κυηφοροῦντος), and permanence together with immortality are  indeed said to be the energies of the demiurge in CH XI, where he is called  Aion, and these energies are used to shape matter into a cosmos.196 It is per- haps overly optimistic to tie together divine epithets of three different texts  into one hypostasis, the demiurgic nous-logos-Aion, born from the womb of  the androgynous father, the self-begotten nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_305">[eref]</span></li>
<li>But the appearance of a noetic  womb also in CH XIII, which through the effluence of the father gives birth to  a new, eternal body for Tat, strengthens the hypothesis: the result of the rebirth  is to make the reborn equal to the demiurge, variously characterized as nous,  logos, Aion, and son of God. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_305">[eref]</span></li>
<li>I  would rather suggest [ⲥⲱ]ⲣ ⲉⲃⲟⲗ, to “spread out, abroad, prepare.”201 The verb  is similar to that suggested by Mahé, but gives us the translation “the one who  set Aion among spirits,” or “prepared Aion by means of spirits.” In other words,  the first nous established the second demiurgic nous, who according to the  Poimandres was born by means of logos and is lord of fire and spirit (CH I, 9). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_306">[eref]</span></li>
<li>§ 13: This interpretation, that the monadic decad should be identified with  Aion, also makes sense of the exclamation of Tat: “Father, I see the All and  myself in the nous.”209 According to Grese, this fits poorly with the foregoing,  and was probably misplaced from its original position, right after the declara- tion of ubiquity in § 11. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_307">[eref]</span></li>
<li>292 Chapter 5 with the nous of the all, Aion. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_308">[eref]</span></li>
<li>From having made himself a stranger to the world, Tat now identifies  himself with the nous of the world, pervading everything. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_308">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Similarly, the rebirth of the essential human is  structurally inverted in relation to the cosmogonic birth of the Poimandres:  The light-nous begat logos without a partner, and sent its offspring down into  matter, while humans must receive the seminal outflowing from God above,  in order for a “new human” to be born. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_309">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The rebirth thus rather corresponds  to the reascent of logos in the second phase of the cosmogony: when logos  descended into matter, the light-nous gave birth to a second, demiurgic nous,  god of fire and spirit, who created the seven planets. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_309">[eref]</span></li>
<li>In response to this, the  logos immediately reascended and merged with the demiurgic nous, leaving  irrational matter behind.215 The ritual rebirth thus mirrors the cosmogony of  Poimandres: The reborn is ritually transformed into logos, which remerges with  the demiurgic nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_309">[eref]</span></li>
<li>It must be that his soul has been “made into essence,” as CH X  predicts of the few souls who will not be bereft of their attendant nous after  death, but who merge with it while they are still alive.221 § 15: Tat now requests to hear the hymn that is sung by the powers of the  Ogdoad, since Hermes had heard Poimandres make predictions (ἐθέσπισε)  about the hymns sung in the Ogdoad earlier.222 This is no doubt an allusion to  CH I, 26, where indeed Poimandres foretells the hymns which will be heard by  the reverent soul that ascends to the Ogdoad after death. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_311">[eref]</span></li>
<li>226    Grese, Corpus Hermeticum XIII, 154, points out that nous only comes to the pure ones in  CH I, 22. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_311">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Hermes  again emphasizes the esoteric nature of this hymn, saying that Tat is only now  allowed to hear it, ἐπὶ τέλει τοῦ παντός, which has mostly been interpreted as “at  the end of it all,” meaning the end of the teaching or the initiation.234 But since  Tat “sees the All, and himself in the nous,” we should probably read ἐπὶ τέλει τοῦ  παντός as indicating again that the new Aionic body of Tat has been completed,  although the initiatory connotation of telos is probably implied too.235 The following passage gives ritual instructions to perform the hymn cor- rectly: “And so, my son, standing in an open space, and facing south at the de- scent of the setting sun, kneel down in prayer, and likewise face east at sunrise. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_313">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Now in the Poimandres, the  birth of fire and spirit follows directly upon the descent of the divine logos  into dark nature (CH I, 5), and the demiurgic nous is the god of fire and  spirit (CH I, 9), and he is the great power that encompasses (περιίσχεσθαι)  fire (CH I, 7). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_321">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Just as Aion encompasses the darkness in the Panaretos,279 the  demiurgic nous in the Poimandres creates seven governors encycling the sen- sible cosmos (CH I, 9–11). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_321">[eref]</span></li>
<li>This again confirms that there is thought to be an essential unity of the reborn  with the demiurgic nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_321">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The allusion to the  Poimandres is pretty much guaranteed by the verb ποιμαίνει in the subsequent  sentence: “For the nous shepherds your logos, spirit-bearer, creator; you are  God!”292 The demiurgic nous “shepherds” the logos and is a bearer of spirit,  meaning that logos and pneuma are his preferred instruments to put creation  in order, and like a good shepherd he guards those who are his. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_324">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Grese claims that this is a  major departure from the view of Poimandres, where humans are not depen- dent of providence, but in CH I, 22–23, we find nous proclaiming that he will  only guard over those who are pure and reverent, so that “they immediately  realize everything, and they give thanks to the father, by regularly blessing him  and singing hymns to him with affection.”298 The wicked, on the other hand,  are left to the punishing demon (τῷ τιμωρῷ δαίμονι). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_325">[eref]</span></li>
<li>From your hymn and your praise my mind has been  illuminated.”299 Scott points out that “the cosmos in me” refers back to “the  All in us,”300 but Hermes corrects Tat by saying that he should have said “the  noetic cosmos,” since he is now identical to the cosmic nous-Aion, and not to  the material cosmos. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_325">[eref]</span></li>
<li>32,  where Hermes discusses the total nous (omnis sensus), the nous of the cosmos  (sensus mundi), and the human nous, and states that the latter is dependent  on its memory: mind stretches down from divinity, through the cosmos to  the humans, and it is because of the tenacity of their memory that humans  297    It is unclear if αἰών should be interpreted impersonally, as eternity or a gnostic eon, or if  the praise is “of Aion,” as translated above, or “from your Aion,” or indeed “from you, Aion.”  Cf. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_325">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The nous of Aion (Aeternitatis sensus) is  then suddenly interposed between the world and God.302 Hermes directs his  gratitude to the highest god (summus deus) who has illuminated him with  the light that permits him to see God, and asks Tat, Asclepius and Hammon  to “hide these divine mysteries in silence among the secrets of your heart  and shield them with reticence.”303 That the hymn of rebirth should also be  kept secret has already been stated (CH XIII, 15), and is repeated in the last  section (§ 22). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_326">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The Coptic version  shows us that for the most part, intellectus equals gnosis, and sensus equals nous (though  in some instances also aisthesis). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_326">[eref]</span></li>
<li>During the rebirth, the candidate is filled  with ten powers, the totality of which can be expressed by the term nous, or  life and light, and during the hymn these powers reach back up towards their  noetic prototypes. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_327">[eref]</span></li>
<li>§ 22: Tat expresses his gratefulness that Hermes approves of his prayer,  whereupon Hermes rejoices that Tat “has bore good fruits from the truth; the  immortal produce.”314 Hermes’ metaphor here recalls the statement in CH IX,  3, that the nous that receives good seeds from God gives birth to good offspring. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_328">[eref]</span></li>
<li>At last, Hermes affirms the successful completion of the rebirth: “You have  mindfully come to know yourself and our father.”319 This is likely to be an allu- sion to the adaptation of the Delphic maxim in the Poimandres: “Let the one  who has nous come to know himself, that he is immortal.”320 Tat has now ful- filled the command of the creator, which he made when he separated the pri- mal human into male and female and thus instituted death. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_329">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The soul and nous of Tat has become  315       Cf. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_329">[eref]</span></li>
<li>314 Chapter 5 life and light, thus reversing the descent and division of the androgynous pri- mal human in CH I, 17–18. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_330">[eref]</span></li>
<li>This motif of an androgynous primal human is of  course to be found in the Platonic tradition, in the jocular anthropogony of  Aristophanes in the Symposium. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_330">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Eve-aisthesis is created from Adam-nous when he sleeps, which is  interpreted so that only when bodily sense is asleep can nous awaken, cf. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_330">[eref]</span></li>
<li>For when I conceived from the  fountain that flowed to me, I gave  birth.17 14     More precisely, it is Tat’s “thought” (ⲡⲁⲙⲉⲉⲩⲉ) that is to be brought up, a noun which is  likely to be a translation of nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_335">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Here we must mention Plutarch’s treatment of the connection be- tween the soul and the moon: “the entity that ascends to the moon after death  is the daimōn, which remains on the lunar surface, and the intellect (nous),  which ascends to the sun.”61 This teaching of Plutarch, who it will be recalled  de savoir dans quel lieu, c’est-à-dire à quelle étape de la voie hermétique la regeneration  se serait produite. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_344">[eref]</span></li>
<li>The  moon is by Plutarch associated with the World-Soul, into which the sun sends  nous, and in the Timaeus the World-Soul was made in a mixing bowl.63 It is  therefore not impossible that the moon is associated with the Hermetic mix- ing bowl full of nous, into which Tat yearns to immerse his soul (CH IV, 6). <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_345">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Likewise, the demiurgic will and nature of the Enneadic nous are mentioned  in order to praise their originator.73 The next stanza is unfortunately mutilated, but as far as we can tell it cred- its God with moving the souls and angels in the Ogdoad, and says something  about his truth and providence, the begetter of everything, and Aion: 55,33  ………ⲉⲃⲟⲗ ⲛ̄ϩⲏⲧϥ̄ ⲉⲩⲕⲓⲙ   … from him are moved 56,1   ⲛ̄ϭⲓ ⲛ̄ⲯⲩⲭⲏ ⲛ̣̄ⲧ̣[ⲙⲁϩϣⲙⲟⲩⲛⲉ]74  the souls of [the eighth] 56,2  [ⲙ︤ⲛ︥] ⲛ̄ⲁⲅⲅⲉⲗⲟⲥ [ⲉⲧⲛ̄ϩⲣⲁⲓ̈ ϩ︤ⲙ︥]   [and] the angels [who are in] 56,3  [ⲡⲉⲓ̈ⲧ]ⲟ̣[ⲡ]ⲟⲥ̣ [·] ⲧ̣[ⲁⲗⲏⲑ]ⲉⲓⲁ     [this place].75 The [tru]th [is only]   ⲟ̣[ⲩⲱ] 56,4     [ⲧ] ϩ︤ⲛ︥ ⲛⲉⲧϣⲟⲟⲡ·76 ⲧ̣ⲉϥⲡ̣[ⲣ]ⲟ̣ⲛⲟ̣[ⲓ]  in those that (really) exist. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_348">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Alberto Camplani proposes  that the meaning of this verb is “emanate,” which seems more than likely: the  first nous has emanated the second demiurgic nous, Aion, by means of spirits. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_349">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Another  possibility would be [ⲡⲉϥϫ]ⲡⲟ [ⲛ̄ⲧⲁϥ]ϫⲡⲟ ⲛ̄ⲟⲩⲟⲛ ⲛⲓⲙ, “it is his birth that has begotten  everything,” reflecting the demiurgical logos-nous as the son of the Enneadic nous, who  in turn begets all things. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_349">[eref]</span></li>
<li>Such a self-image is also reflected in the Definitions of Asclepius to King  Ammon (CH XVI), in the famous disparagement of the Greek language in favor  of Egyptian which may have something to do with magical vowels and nomina  barbara: “The logos that is expressed in our ancestral language keeps the nous  of the logoi clear. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_365">[eref]</span></li>
<li>ⲁⲛⲟⲕ ⲡ̣ⲉ̣ [ⲡⲛⲟ]ⲩ̣[ⲥ ⲁⲩⲱ] ϯ ⲛⲁⲩ     I am [no]u[s and] I see another     ⲉⲕⲉⲛⲟⲩⲥ ⲡⲉⲧⲕ[ⲓⲙ] ⲉⲧⲯⲩⲭⲏ·    nous, the one that moves the soul. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_372">[eref]</span></li>
<li>ⲁⲉⲓϫⲟⲟⲥ ⲱ̄ ⲡⲁϣⲏⲣⲉ ϫⲉ ⲁⲛⲟⲕ ⲡⲉ   I have said, my son, that I am nous. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_373">[eref]</span></li>
<li>ⲁⲛⲟⲕ ⲇⲉ ⲡⲛⲟⲩⲥ ϯⲣ̄ⲛⲟⲉⲓ·     And as for me, the nous,        I understand. <span class="entity-pill" title="Source fragment: christian_h_bull_the_tradition_of_hermes_trismegistus_the_egyptian_priestly_figure_as_a_teacher_of_hellenized_wisdom_br:page_373">[eref]</span></li>
